<p>The <b>Assyrian genocide</b> (also known as <i><b>Sayfo</b></i> or <i><b>Seyfo</b></i>, ("Sword") <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="syr" xml:lang="syr">ܩܛܠܥܡܐ ܣܘܪܝܝܐ</span>‎ or <span lang="syr" xml:lang="syr">ܣܝܦܐ</span>) refers to the mass slaughter of the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian</a> population of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> and those in neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Qajar_dynasty" title="Qajar dynasty">Persia</a> by <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Army_(1861%E2%80%931922)" title="Ottoman Army (1861–1922)">Ottoman troops</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.nl_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.nl-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexander_Laban_Hinton_pp_117_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_Laban_Hinton_pp_117-2">[2]</a></sup> during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_World_War" title="First World War">First World War</a>, in conjunction with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armenian_genocide" title="Armenian genocide">Armenian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">Greek genocides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anahit_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Anahit-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Assyrian civilian population of upper <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Tur_Abdin" title="Tur Abdin">Tur Abdin</a> region, the <a href="/wiki/Hakk%C3%A2ri" title="Hakkâri">Hakkâri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Van_Province" title="Van Province">Van</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Siirt_Province" title="Siirt Province">Siirt</a> provinces of present-day southeastern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Urmia" title="Urmia">Urmia</a> region of northwestern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>) was forcibly relocated and massacred by the <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_people" title="Ottoman people">Ottoman</a> (<a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turkish</a>) army, together with other armed and allied Muslim peoples, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" title="Kurdish people">Kurds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chechens" title="Chechens">Chechens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a>, between 1914 and 1920, with further attacks on unarmed fleeing civilians conducted by local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> militias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Assyrian genocide took place in the same context as the Armenian and Greek genocides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Since the Assyrian genocide took place within the context of the much more widespread Armenian genocide, scholarship treating it as a separate event is scarce, with the exceptions of the works of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Yacoub" title="Joseph Yacoub">Joseph Yacoub</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Gabriele Yonan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> David Gaunt and Hannibal Travis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anahit_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Anahit-5">[5]</a></sup> who have classified the genocide as a systematic campaign by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Young_Turk" title="Young Turk">Young Turk</a> government. Other scholars, such as Hilmar Kaiser, <a href="/wiki/Donald_Bloxham" title="Donald Bloxham">Donald Bloxham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taner_Ak%C3%A7am" title="Taner Akçam">Taner Akçam</a> have differing opinions with regards to the extent of governmental involvement and systematic nature of the genocide, asserting a less systematic policy and different treatment in comparison to the Armenians.</p>
<p>Unlike the Armenians, there were no orders to deport Assyrians. The attacks against them were not of standardized nature and incorporated various methods of massacre; in some cities, all Assyrian men were slain and the others were forced to flee. These massacres were often carried out upon the initiatives of local politicians and Kurdish tribes, and Assyrian collaboration with Russians prompted some of them. Exposure, disease and starvation during the flight of Assyrians increased the death toll, and women were subjected to widespread <a href="/wiki/Sexual_abuse" title="Sexual abuse">sexual abuse</a> in some areas.</p>
<p>Estimates on the overall death toll have varied. Providing detailed statistics of the various estimates of the Churches' population after the genocide, David Gaunt accepts the figure of 275,000 deaths as reported by the Assyrian delegation at the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Lausanne" title="Treaty of Lausanne">Treaty of Lausanne</a> and ventures that the death toll would be around 300,000 because of uncounted Assyrian-inhabited areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Assyrian_Genocide_of_1915_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Assyrian_Genocide_of_1915-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rudolph_Rummel" title="Rudolph Rummel">Rudolph Rummel</a> gives the number of Christian deaths in Assyrian-populated regions of Turkey as 102,000 and adds to this the killing of around 47,000 Assyrians in Persia.</p>
<p>In 2007, the <a href="/wiki/International_Association_of_Genocide_Scholars" title="International Association of Genocide Scholars">International Association of Genocide Scholars</a> (IAGS) reached a consensus that the "Ottoman campaign against Christian minorities of the Empire between 1914 and 1923 constituted a genocide against Armenians, Assyrians, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontian_Greeks" title="Pontian Greeks">Pontian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Greeks" title="Ottoman Greeks">Anatolian Greeks</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The IAGS referred to the work of Gaunt and Travis in passing this resolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Stanton" title="Gregory Stanton">Gregory Stanton</a>, the President of the IAGS in 2007–2008 and the founder of <a href="/wiki/Genocide_Watch" title="Genocide Watch">Genocide Watch</a>, endorsed the "repudiation by the world's leading genocide scholars of the Turkish government's ninety-year denial of the Ottoman Empire's genocides against its Christian populations, including Assyrians, Greeks, and Armenians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span></h2>
<p>The Assyrian genocide is sometimes also referred to as <i>Sayfo</i> or <i>Seyfo</i> in English language sources, based on the modern Assyrian (Mesopotamian neo-Aramaic) designation <i>Saypā</i> (<span lang="arc" xml:lang="arc">ܣܝܦܐ</span>), "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saif" title="Saif">sword</a>", pronounced as <i>Seyfo</i>, and as <i>Sayfo</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Western_Neo-Aramaic" title="Western Neo-Aramaic">Western dialect</a> (the term abbreviates <i>shato d'sayfo</i> "year of the sword"). The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Aramaic" title="Neo-Aramaic">Assyrian</a> name <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Aramaic transliteration">Qeṭlā ḏ-‘Amā Āṯûrāyā</span></i> (<span lang="arc" xml:lang="arc">ܩܛܠܐ ܕܥܡܐ ܐܬܘܪܝܐ</span>), which literally means "killing of the Assyrian people", is used by some groups to describe these events. The word <i>Qṭolamo</i> (<span lang="arc" xml:lang="arc">ܩܛܠܥܡܐ</span>) which means <i>Genocide</i> is also used in Assyrian diaspora media. The term used in <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a> media is <i>Süryani Soykırımı</i>.</p>
<p>In countries where significant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_diaspora" title="Assyrian diaspora">Assyrian diaspora</a> communities exist, the designation "Assyrian" has become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_naming_controversy" title="Assyrian naming controversy">controversial</a>, notably in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrians/Syriacs_in_Germany" title="Assyrians/Syriacs in Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assyrians/Syriacs_in_Sweden" title="Assyrians/Syriacs in Sweden">Sweden</a>, alternative terms such as <i>Assyriska/syrianska/kaldeiska folkmordet</i> "Assyrian/Syriac/Chaldean genocide" are employed. Nestorians, Syrians, Syriacs, and Chaldeans were names imposed by Western missionaries such as the Catholics and Protestants on the Ottoman and Persian Assyrians. The Greek, Persian, and Arab rulers of occupied Assyria, as well as Chaldean and Syrian Orthodox patriarchs, priests, and monks, and Armenian, British, and French laypeople, called them all Assyrians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_population_1914.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="806" data-file-width="1062" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Assyrian_population_1914.svg/220px-Assyrian_population_1914.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Assyrian_population_1914.svg/330px-Assyrian_population_1914.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Assyrian_population_1914.svg/440px-Assyrian_population_1914.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_population_1914.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Percentage of Assyrian populations in Several Vilayets and Sanjaks in the Ottoman Empire and Urmia in Persia prior to WW1 as presented by the Assyrian delegation to the 1919 peace conference.
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #8800aa; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> More than 50%</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #cc00ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 30–40%</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #d42aff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 20–30%</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #dd55ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 10–20%</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #e580ff; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 5–10%</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Assyrian population in the Ottoman Empire numbered about one million at the turn of the twentieth century and was largely concentrated in what is now <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup> However, researchers such as David Gaunt have noted that the Assyrian population was around 600,000 prior to World War I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Assyrian_Genocide_of_1915_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Assyrian_Genocide_of_1915-9">[9]</a></sup> There were also hundreds of thousands of Maronite Christians in Lebanon, with some Assyrian heritage but which are less often called Assyrians. There were significantly larger communities located in the regions near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Urmia" title="Lake Urmia">Lake Urmia</a> in Persia, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Van" title="Lake Van">Lake Van</a> (specifically the <a href="/wiki/Hakkari" title="Hakkari">Hakkari</a> region) and <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, as well as the eastern Ottoman <a href="/wiki/Vilayet" title="Vilayet">vilayets</a> of <a href="/wiki/Diyarbekir_Vilayet" title="Diyarbekir Vilayet">Diyarbekir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Erzurum_Vilayet" title="Erzurum Vilayet">Erzurum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bitlis_Vilayet" title="Bitlis Vilayet">Bitlis</a>. Like other Christians residing in the empire, they were treated as second-class citizens and denied public positions of power. Violence directed against them prior to the First World War was not new. Many Assyrians were subjected to <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">Kurdish</a> brigandage and even outright massacre and forced conversion to Islam, as was the case of the Assyrians of Hakkari during the <a href="/wiki/Massacres_of_Badr_Khan" title="Massacres of Badr Khan">massacres of Badr Khan</a> in the 1840s and the <a href="/wiki/Massacres_of_Diyarbak%C4%B1r_(1895)" title="Massacres of Diyarbakır (1895)">Massacres of Diyarbakır</a> during the 1895–96 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamidian_Massacres" title="Hamidian Massacres">Hamidian Massacres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anahit_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Anahit-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hamidiye_(cavalry)" title="Hamidiye (cavalry)">Hamidiye</a> received assurances from the Ottoman Sultan that they could kill Assyrians and Armenians with impunity, and were particularly active in <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Eanl%C4%B1urfa" title="Şanlıurfa">Urhoy</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amid" title="Amid">Diyarbakir</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outbreak_of_war">Outbreak of war</span></h3>
<p>The Ottoman Empire began massacring Assyrians in the nineteenth century, a time of friendly relations between the Ottomans and the British, who were defending the Ottomans from the Russian Empire's efforts to include under its protection the communities of Ottoman Orthodox Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup> In October 1914, the Ottoman Empire began deporting and massacring Assyrians and Armenians in <a href="/wiki/Van" title="Van">Van</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-6"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup> After attacking Russian cities and declaring war on Britain and France, the Empire declared a holy war on Christians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-7"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup> The German Kaiser and the German Ambassador to the Ottoman Empire directed and orchestrated the holy war, and financed the Ottomans' war against the Russian Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-8"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Responsibility_of_the_Ottoman_government">Responsibility of the Ottoman government</span></h3>
<p>The degree of the responsibility of the Ottoman government and whether the genocide had systematic nature has been the subject of different scholarly opinions. Concerning the responsibility of the Ottoman government, Hilmar Kaiser wrote that Talaat Pasha ordered the deportations of the Assyrians in the area on 26 October 1914, fearing their collaboration with the advancing Russian troops, but the order was postponed and abandoned three days later due to a lack of forces. When the Assyrians did not collaborate with Russians, any plans to deport them were cancelled. Kaiser wrote that the massacres of Assyrians were apparently not a part of the official Ottoman policy and that the Assyrians were ordered to be treated differently from the Armenians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Taner_Ak%C3%A7am" title="Taner Akçam">Taner Akçam</a>, a leading specialist in the Armenian Genocide, cites Ottoman official correspondence in 1919, inquiring the number and conditions of Assyrians deported, to state that the Ottoman government was unaware of the full numerical extent of the deportations of Assyrians. Another Ottoman document orders Assyrians to be detained in their present locations, instead of their deportation, which, according to Akçam, indicates that the Assyrian population could have been treated differently from the Armenians, but that they were often "eliminated" alongside them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-akcamsyr_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-akcamsyr-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Donald_Bloxham" title="Donald Bloxham">Donald Bloxham</a>, a genocide scholar, stated that while Assyrians of western Persia, Hakkari, Bitlis, Van and Diarbekir were massacred along with Armenians, they were "not subject to the same systematic destruction".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dominik J. Schaller and Jürgen Zimmerer wrote that due to the lack of an international diaspora and a nation state, the Assyrian were perceived as more vulnerable and less threatening by the Young Turks, which led to their extermination being "less systematic". Massacres of Assyrians were often undertaken through the initiatives of local officials and groups. Nevertheless, they classified the campaign against Assyrians as having "genocidal quality".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ernst_II,_Prince_of_Hohenlohe-Langenburg" title="Ernst II, Prince of Hohenlohe-Langenburg">Ernst II, Prince of Hohenlohe-Langenburg</a>, the German special envoy in Constantinople, sent a report describing "systematic extermination" of the Christian population of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diarbekir" title="Diarbekir">Diarbekir</a> province by Reshid Bey, the governor. Martin Tamcke wrote that a German chargé d'affairs in Constantinople sent to the German Chancellery an article from a Young Turk-controlled newspaper, which mentioned the expulsion of Assyrians in the east as an example of the "cleansing of the empire of Christian elements". Tamcke wrote that documents such as these, along with oral traditions, are evidence of a systematic policy of extermination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>David Gaunt compared the attacks on Assyrians in <a href="/wiki/Hakkari" title="Hakkari">Hakkari</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diarbekir" title="Diarbekir">Diarbekir</a>, and wrote that while the former was mainly perpetrated upon the orders of the Turkish government, the latter was a local initiative of CUP politicians unconnected with the central government, and with no orders to exterminate Assyrians in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gauntind_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-gauntind-18">[18]</a></sup></p>

<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Persecutions_of_the_Catholic_Church_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_characteristics">General characteristics</span></h3>
<p>According to historian David Gaunt, a primary characteristic was the total targeting of the Assyrian population, including farming villages as well as rebelling mountain tribes. The killing in rural regions was more extensive, while some survived the massacres in cities; Gaunt states that this indicates that a primary aim was the confiscation of land. The property, villages and animals of the villagers were destroyed totally to prevent their return. Gaunt states that organized troops were tasked with killing and expelling Assyrians in <a href="/wiki/Hakkari" title="Hakkari">Hakkari</a> and Ottoman-controlled parts of Persia, as well as resisting villages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> There were also deportations of Assyrians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-akcamsyr_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-akcamsyr-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gaunt wrote that there was no standardized way of killing. He cites accounts of killings at town halls, river rafts, tunnels, streets, and during the flight of the victims. The methods included stabbing, decapitation, drowning, shooting and stoning among others according to eyewitness accounts cited by Gaunt; these accounts also record local officers having collections of body parts, such as ears, noses and "female body parts".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-intro-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Percy_Sykes" title="Percy Sykes">Percy Sykes</a>, a British officer in Persia, wrote that the Assyrians would have been exterminated if they had not fled to Persia. However, starvation, disease and fatigue cost the lives of 65,000 more Assyrians on their way to Persia or once they had arrived there, according to Christoph Baumer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-intro-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diyarbekir">Diyarbekir</span></h3>
<p>The earliest programs of extermination took place in the southern province of Diyarbekir, under the leadership of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Re%C5%9Fit_Bey" title="Reşit Bey">Reshid Bey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anahit_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Anahit-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> A German Vice-Consul reported in July 1915 that Assyrians were being massacred in <a href="/wiki/Diyarbekir_Vilayet" title="Diyarbekir Vilayet">Diyarbekir Vilayet</a>. A German Consul reported in September 1915 that the adult Christians of <a href="/wiki/Diyarbak%C4%B1r" title="Diyarbakır">Diyarbakır</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Harput" title="Harput">Harput</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mardin" title="Mardin">Mardin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Viran%C5%9Fehir" title="Viranşehir">Viranşehir</a> had been targeted, and also mentioned an Ottoman reign of terror in <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Eanl%C4%B1urfa" title="Şanlıurfa">Urhoy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-9"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup> According to the reports, the Assyrian population of <a href="/wiki/Faysh_Khabur" title="Faysh Khabur">Faysh Khabur</a> was completely killed, along with all the male Assyrians of Mardin and Siirt. The widows and orphans of these men were reportedly left to flee to <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a> on foot, and died on their way due to starvation and harsh conditions. These atrocities prompted the Assyrian patriarch to appeal to the Russian representative in the Caucasus, claiming that the Turkish leaders were intent on killing all Assyrians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The German ambassador reported that the Ottoman Empire was being "clear[ed]" of its indigenous Christians by "eliminat[ion]".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-10"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup> In July 1915, he confirmed that the Assyrians of Midyat, Nisibis, and Jazirah were also slain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-11"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the Syrian Patriarchate, the Turkish government ordered an attack on the Christian villages near Mardin, which were mostly inhabited by Assyrians. The soldiers went beyond attacking property and killed civilians, for instance, the Assyrians of <a href="/wiki/K%C4%B1z%C4%B1ltepe" title="Kızıltepe">Kızıltepe/Tell Armen</a> were gathered in a church and burned. In Diyarbekir, women and children were deported, but only a very small number reached their destinations as women were killed, raped or sold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Individual accounts of the massacres include several villages. In the village of Cherang near Diyarbekir, 114 men were killed and the women and children were put to forced agricultural labor and given the choice to convert or die. The massacre was committed by an Al-Khamsin death squad, which were recruited by the government and led by officials, while composed of local urban Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In the village of Hanewiye, about 400 Assyrians are believed to have been murdered. In Hassana, a village near <a href="/wiki/Cizre" title="Cizre">Jezire</a>, the 300 inhabitants were massacred, with some managing to survive and flee. The inhabitants of the village of Kavel-Karre were attacked by Kurdish tribes on 19 June 1915 and killed; their bodies were then thrown into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tigris_River" title="Tigris River">Tigris River</a>. In Kafarbe, 2 km from the <a href="/wiki/Mor_Gabriel_Monastery" title="Mor Gabriel Monastery">Mor Gabriel Monastery</a>, 200 Assyrians were attacked by a clan of Kurds and murdered in 1917. However, there were also cases when those in power chose to protect the Assyrians, as Rachid Osman, the agha of <a href="/wiki/%C5%9E%C4%B1rnak" title="Şırnak">Şırnak</a> protected the 300–500 inhabitants of Harbol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>In their book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Treatment_of_Armenians_in_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="The Treatment of Armenians in the Ottoman Empire">The Treatment of Armenians in the Ottoman Empire</a></i>, Viscount Bryce and Arnold Toynbee included a letter from the Presbyterian American Church in Urmia, sent on 6 March 1916, which related information from a survivor of the events described. In the document, it is written that nearly all of the 30,000 Assyrians (called "Nestorians") of the <a href="/wiki/Bohtan" title="Bohtan">Bohtan</a> region had been massacred by the Kurds and Turkish soldiers with the orders of the government. While some Kurdish leaders tried to protect the population, they were unable to as the order had allegedly come from the government and such friendly acts were punished. All Christian villages of the plain were reportedly "wiped out", including three Protestant villages. In Monsoria, one of these villages, Assyrian women reportedly jumped into the Tigris River to prevent their capture by the Kurds. The surviving women and children were taken as captives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Figures by the Syriac Orthodox Patriarchate presented to the peace conference after the war state that 77,963 Assyrians were killed in 278 villages of the Diyarbekir province.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Jean Naayem writes that about 50 villages close to <a href="/wiki/Midyat" title="Midyat">Midyat</a> were ruined and their Assyrian inhabitants slaughtered, but he does not name any of them nor give any casualty figures. However, the figure agrees with the data of the patriarchate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Van">Van</span></h3>
<p>In October 1914, 71 Assyrian men of <a href="/wiki/Y%C3%BCksekova" title="Yüksekova">Yüksekova/Gawar</a> were arrested and taken to the local government center in <a href="/wiki/Ba%C5%9Fkale" title="Başkale">Başkale</a> and killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bryce_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-bryce-29">[29]</a></sup> In November 1914, Russian troops briefly occupied the towns of Başkale and Saray, following their retreat, the Assyrian and Armenian populations of these areas were accused of collaboration and targeted for revenge. According to eyewitness accounts collected by Russians and local observers, at least twelve villages were "wiped out" in this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jevdet Pasha the governor of Van, is reported to have held a meeting in February 1915 at which he said, "We have cleansed the Armenians and Syriac [Christian]s from Azerbaijan, and we will do the same in Van."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In late 1915, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jevdet_Bey" title="Jevdet Bey">Jevdet Bey</a>, Military Governor of <a href="/wiki/Van_Vilayet" title="Van Vilayet">Van Vilayet</a>, upon entering <a href="/wiki/Siirt" title="Siirt">Siirt</a> (or Seert) with 8,000 soldiers whom he himself called "The Butchers' Battalion" (<a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>: <span lang="tr" xml:lang="tr"><i>Kasap Taburu</i></span>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anahit_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Anahit-5">[5]</a></sup> ordered the massacre of almost 20,000 Assyrian <a href="/wiki/Civilian" title="Civilian">civilians</a> in at least 30 villages.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The same "butcher battalions" killed all the male Assyrian and Armenian population of <a href="/wiki/Bitlis" title="Bitlis">Bitlis</a>. They reportedly raped the women, and subsequently sold them or gave them as "gifts".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Travis-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The town of Sa'irt/Seert (modern-day Siirt), was populated by Assyrians and Armenians. Seert was the seat of a Chaldean Archbishop <a href="/wiki/Addai_Scher" title="Addai Scher">Addai Scher</a> who was murdered by the Kurds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The eyewitness Hyacinthe Simon wrote that 4,000 Christians died in Seert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaunt.2C_David_2006.2C_p._436_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaunt.2C_David_2006.2C_p._436-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> According to Joseph Naayem, who was an Assyrian priest, the number of Assyrians killed in the town of Seert/Siirt alone exceeded 8000. Eyewitness accounts state that the Assyrian men were rounded up by criminal gangs and forced to a march to the valley of Zeryabe, where they were killed. This was followed by the gangs' attack on women. The Ottoman officer Raphael de Nogales described a "slope [...] crowned by thousands of half-nude and still bleeding corpses, lying in heaps". He then wrote that when he entered Siirt, he saw that the police and the locals were sacking Christian homes, and learned that the governors of the town directed the massacre, which had been arranged beforehand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-intro-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to "the Blue Book" of the American government, widespread ethnic cleansing and massacres occurred against the Assyrians as well as Armenians in the <a href="/wiki/Hakkari" title="Hakkari">Hakkari</a> area, with the orders for the deportations of Armenians being misinterpreted as orders against all Christians by the local Kurds. It was reported that an attack was launched on Assyrian dwellings in summer 1915, and that Assyrians were attempted to be "starved out". According to Paul Shimmon and <a href="/wiki/Arnold_J._Toynbee" title="Arnold J. Toynbee">Arnold J. Toynbee</a>, an Assyrian village called "Goele", with the population of 300, was attacked and its men were killed, while the women and children were forced into slavery and the houses were pillaged. In another village with fifty houses, the Kurds reportedly killed the entire civilian population. "The Blue Book" states that in one district of Hakkari, only 17 Christian survivors were left from 41 villages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Travis-32">[32]</a></sup> In April 1915, after a number of failed Kurdish attempts, Ottoman Troops invaded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gawar" title="Gawar">Gawar</a>, a region of Hakkari, and massacred the entire population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> There were later reports of the mass killing of hundreds of Assyrians in the same area, and women being forced into sexual slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Travis-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>David Gaunt wrote that the Assyrians of the Hakkari area were targeted in a "full ethnic cleansing" and asserted that they "faced the full wrath of the Ottoman government as well as the local Kurdish tribes". He claimed that due to their consistent contact and collaboration with the Russians, they were targeted with atrocities, and after a battle in which they collaborated with the Russians to defeat the Ottoman Army, the army perpetrated the massacres against Christians in Başkale, Siirt and Bitlis described above. Talaat Pasha also allegedly ordered a policy in which Ottoman troops, with the support of Kurdish tribes, defeated Assyrians and drove them to the mountains, subsequently destroying their property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gauntind_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-gauntind-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assyrian_resistance_in_upper_Mesopotamia">Assyrian resistance in upper Mesopotamia</span></h3>
<p>On March 3, 1918, the Ottoman army led by Kurdish soldiers assassinated one of the most important Assyrian leaders at the time. This resulted in the retaliation of the Assyrians. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Malik_Yosip_Khoshaba&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Malik Yosip Khoshaba (page does not exist)">Malik Yosip Khoshaba</a> of the Bit-Tiyari tribe led a successful attack against the Ottomans. Assyrian forces in the region also attacked the Kurdish fortress of <a href="/wiki/Simko_Shikak" title="Simko Shikak">Simko Shikak</a>, the leader who had assassinated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mar_Shimun_XIX_Benyamin" title="Mar Shimun XIX Benyamin">Mar Shimun XIX Benyamin</a>, they successfully stormed it, defeating the Kurds, however Simko escaped and fled.</p>
<p>Assyrians were involved in a number of clashes in Turkey with Ottoman forces, including <a href="/wiki/Kurds" title="Kurds">Kurds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a> loyal to the empire. When armed and in sufficient numbers they were able to defend themselves successfully. However, they were often cut off in small pockets, vastly outnumbered and surrounded, and unarmed villagers made easy targets for Ottoman and Kurdish forces.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Events_in_Persia">Events in Persia</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_campaign" title="Persian campaign">Persian campaign</a></div>
<p>The Assyrians in Persia armed themselves under the command of General <a href="/wiki/Agha_Petros" title="Agha Petros">Agha Petros</a>, who had been approached by the Allies to help fight the Ottomans.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (February 2015)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> They put up a resistance, and Agha Petros' volunteer army had quite a few successes over the Ottoman forces and their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish</a> allies, notably at Suldouze where 1,500 Assyrian horsemen overcame the far larger Ottoman force of over 8,000, commanded by Kheiri Bey. Agha Petros also defeated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turks" title="Ottoman Turks">Ottoman Turks</a> in a major engagement at Sauj Bulak and drove them back to Rowanduz. Assyrian forces in Persia were greatly affected by the withdrawal of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> from the war and the collapse of <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenian</a> armed resistance in the region. They were left cut off, with no supplies, vastly outnumbered and surrounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-12"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Urmia_and_surroundings">Urmia and surroundings</span></h3>
<p>The Ottoman Empire invaded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_Azerbaijan" title="Iranian Azerbaijan">northwestern</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> in 1914.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-13"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup> Before the end of 1914, Turkish and Kurdish troops had successfully entered the villages in and around <a href="/wiki/Urmia" title="Urmia">Urmia</a>. On February 21, 1915 the Turkish army in Urmia seized 61 leading Assyrians from the <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> <a href="/wiki/Mission_(station)" title="Mission (station)">missions</a> as hostages, demanding large ransoms. The mission had enough money to convince the Ottomans to let 20 of the men go. However, on February 22 the remaining 41 were executed, having their heads cut off at the stairs of the Charbachsh Gate. The dead included bishop Mar Denkha.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:215px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ruins_of_Gulpashan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="133" data-file-width="213" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1a/Ruins_of_Gulpashan.jpg" width="213"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ruins_of_Gulpashan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Old picture depicting the ruins of the Assyrian village of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golpashan" title="Golpashan">Golpashan</a> (<a href="/wiki/West_Azerbaijan_Province" title="West Azerbaijan Province">West Azerbaijan Province</a>, Iran) after its destruction by the invading Ottoman army.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most of the Assyrian villages were unarmed. The only protection they had was when the Russian army finally took control of the area, years after the presence of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman army</a> had been removed. On February 25, 1915, Ottoman troops stormed their way into the villages of Gulpashan and Salamas. Almost the entire village of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golpashan" title="Golpashan">Golpashan</a>, of a population of 2,500, were massacred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anahit_5-5"><a href="#cite_note-Anahit-5">[5]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Salmas" title="Salmas">Salmas</a>, about 750 Armenian and Assyrian refugees were protected by Iranian civilians in the village. The commander of the Ottoman division stormed the houses despite the fact that Persians lived in them, and roped all the men together in large groups and forced them to march in the fields between Khusrawa and Haftevan/Hafdewan. The men were shot or killed in other ways. The protection of Christians by local Persian civilians is also confirmed in the 1915 British report: "Many Moslems tried to save their Christian neighbours and offered them shelter in their houses, but the Turkish authorities were implacable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bryce_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-bryce-29">[29]</a></sup> According to American official accounts, the largest Assyrian village in the Urmia region was overrun and all its men killed, while the women were attacked. In Haftevan, the Russian troops later discovered more than 700 corpses, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i> also claimed the abduction of 500 Assyrian girls. According to similar reports, 200 Assyrians were killed by burning in a church.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Travis-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the winter of 1915, 4,000 Assyrians died from disease, hunger, and exposure, and about 1000 were killed in the villages of Urmia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-14"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Times" title="Los Angeles Times">Los Angeles Times</a></i>, in Urmia alone, 800 Assyrians were massacred and 2000 died from disease. American documents report widespread sexual violence against Assyrian women of all ages and the looting and destruction of the houses of about five-sixth of the Assyrian population. Reports state that over 200 girls were forced into sexual slavery and conversion into Islam. Eugene Griselle from the Ethnological Society of Paris gives the figure of 8,500 for the number of deaths in the Urmia region; according to other reports, out of an Assyrian population of 30,000, one-fifth was killed, their villages and churches destroyed. An English priest in the area estimates the death toll at 6,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-Travis-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, David Gaunt wrote that the massacres were reciprocated by the Assyrians. Assyrian Jilu tribes were accused of committing massacres of local villagers in the plains of Salmas; local Iranian officials reported that between <a href="/wiki/Khoy" title="Khoy">Khoi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jolfa,_Iran_(city)" title="Jolfa, Iran (city)">Julfa</a>, a great number of villagers were massacred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1918, the Assyrian population of Urmia was nearly wiped out, 1,000 killed in the French and American mission buildings, 200 surrounding villages destroyed, and thousands perished of famine, disease, and forced marches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-15"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early 1918, many Assyrians started to flee present-day Turkey. Mar Shimun Benyamin had arranged for some 3,500 Assyrians to reside in the district of <a href="/wiki/Khoy" title="Khoy">Khoi</a>. Not long after settling in, Kurdish troops of the Ottoman Army massacred the population almost entirely. One of the few that survived was Reverend John Eshoo. After escaping, he stated:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>You have undoubtedly heard of the Assyrian massacre of Khoi, but I am certain you do not know the details.<br/>
<br/>
These Assyrians were assembled into one caravansary, and shot to death by guns and revolvers. Blood literally flowed in little streams, and the entire open space within the caravansary became a pool of crimson liquid. The place was too small to hold all the living victims waiting for execution. They were brought in groups, and each new group was compelled to stand over the heap of the still bleeding bodies and shot to death. The fearful place became literally a human slaughter house, receiving its speechless victims, in groups of ten and twenty at a time, for execution.<br/>
<br/>
At the same time, the Assyrians, who were residing in the suburb of the city, were brought together and driven into the spacious courtyard of a house [...] The Assyrian refugees were kept under guard for eight days, without anything to eat. At last they were removed from their place of confinement and taken to a spot prepared for their brutal killing. These helpless Assyrians marched like lambs to their slaughter, and they opened not their mouth, save by sayings "Lord, into thy hands we commit our spirits. [...]<br/>
<br/>
The executioners began by cutting first the fingers of their victims, join by joint, till the two hands were entirely amputated. Then they were stretched on the ground, after the manner of the animals that are slain in the Fast, but these with their faces turned upward, and their heads resting upon the stones or blocks of wood Then their throats were half cut, so as to prolong their torture of dying, and while struggling in the agony of death, the victims were kicked and clubbed by heavy poles the murderers carried Many of them, while still laboring under the pain of death, were thrown into ditches and buried before their souls had expired.<br/>
<br/>
The young men and the able-bodied men were separated from among the very young and the old. They were taken some distance from the city and used as targets by the shooters. They all fell, a few not mortally wounded. One of the leaders went to the heaps of the fallen and shouted aloud, swearing by the names of Islam's prophets that those who had not received mortal wounds should rise and depart, as they would not be harmed any more. A few, thus deceived, stood up, but only to fall this time killed by another volley from the guns of the murderers.<br/>
<br/>
Some of the younger and good looking women, together with a few little girls of attractive appearance, pleaded to be killed. Against their will were forced into Islam's harems. Others were subjected to such fiendish insults that I cannot possibly describe. Death, however, came to their rescue and saved them from the vile passions of the demons. The death toll of Assyrians totaled 2,770 men, women and children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Werda_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Werda-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Baquba_camps">Baquba camps</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Reports_of_the_missionary_and_benevolent_boards_and_committees_to_the_General_Assembly_of_the_Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America_(1919)_(14760129156).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2402" data-file-width="1570" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Reports_of_the_missionary_and_benevolent_boards_and_committees_to_the_General_Assembly_of_the_Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America_%281919%29_%2814760129156%29.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Reports_of_the_missionary_and_benevolent_boards_and_committees_to_the_General_Assembly_of_the_Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America_%281919%29_%2814760129156%29.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Reports_of_the_missionary_and_benevolent_boards_and_committees_to_the_General_Assembly_of_the_Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America_%281919%29_%2814760129156%29.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Reports_of_the_missionary_and_benevolent_boards_and_committees_to_the_General_Assembly_of_the_Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America_(1919)_(14760129156).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/William_Ambrose_Shedd" title="William Ambrose Shedd">Dr. Shedd</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By mid-1918, the British army had convinced the Ottomans to let them have access to about 30,000 Assyrians from various parts of Persia. The British decided to relocate all 30,000 from Persia to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baquba" title="Baquba">Baquba</a>, northern Iraq, in the hope that this would prevent further massacres. Many others had already left for northern Iraq after the Russian withdrawal and collapse of Armenian lines. The transferring took just 25 days, but at least 7,000 of them had died during the trip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Some died of exposure, hunger or disease, other civilians fell prey to attacks from armed bands of Kurds and Arabs. At Baquba, Assyrians were forced to defend themselves from further Arab and Kurdish raids, which they were able to do successfully.</p>
<p>A memorandum from American Presbyterian Missionaries at Urmia During the Great War 16 to British Minister Sir Percy Cox had this to say:</p>
<blockquote>
<p>Capt. Gracey doubtless talked rather big in the hopes of putting heart into the Assyrians and holding up this front against the Turks. [Consequently,] We have met all the orders issued by the late <a href="/wiki/William_Ambrose_Shedd" title="William Ambrose Shedd">Dr. Shedd</a> which have been presented to us and a very large number of Assyrian refugees are being maintained at Baquba, chiefly at H.M.G.'s expense.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>In 1920, the British decided to close down the Baquba camps. The majority of Assyrians of the camp decided to go back to the Hakkari mountains, while the rest were dispersed throughout Iraq, where there was already an Assyrian community.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, they would again be targeted there in the 1933 <a href="/wiki/Simele_massacre" title="Simele massacre">Simele massacre</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_toll">Death toll</span></h2>
<p>Scholars have summarized events as follows: specific massacres included 25,000 Assyrians in Midyat, 21,000 in Jezira-ibn-Omar, 7,000 in Nisibis, 7,000 in Urfa, 7,000 in the Qudshanis region, 6,000 in Mardin, 5,000 in Diyarbekir, 4,000 in Adana, 4,000 in Brahimie, and 3,500 in Harput.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Anahit_5-6"><a href="#cite_note-Anahit-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-Travis-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> In its December 4, 1922, memorandum, the Assyro-Chaldean National Council stated that the total death toll was unknown. It estimated that about 275,000 "Assyro-Chaldeans" died between 1914 and 1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yacoub_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yacoub-43">[43]</a></sup> The population of the Assyrians of the Ottoman Empire and Persia was about 600,000 before the genocide, and was reduced by 275,000, with very few survivors in 1930s Turkey or Iran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis2_4-16"><a href="#cite_note-Travis2-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Contemporary newspapers reported death tolls of 200,000 to 250,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-Travis-32">[32]</a></sup> Representatives from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" title="Anglican Church">Anglican Church</a> in the region claimed that about half of the Assyrian population had perished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intro_20-3"><a href="#cite_note-intro-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The memorandum of the Assyrian Archbishopric of Syria (<a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>-<a href="/wiki/Homs" title="Homs">Homs</a>) to the 1920 peace conference, places the death toll at 90,313 people, with 345 villages burned and 156 churches destroyed. The archbishop demanded 250,000 pounds sterling of reparations to compensate for the destruction of the churches. The figures of the archbishopric places the death toll in Harput at 3,500, in <a href="/wiki/Midyat" title="Midyat">Midyat</a> at 25,830, in Diyarbekir and surroundings at 5,679, in Jezireh at 7,510, in <a href="/wiki/Nusaybin" title="Nusaybin">Nusaybin</a> at 7,000, in Mardin at 5,815, in <a href="/wiki/Bitlis" title="Bitlis">Bitlis</a> at 850, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urfa" title="Urfa">Urfa</a> at 340, and tens of thousands at other areas. The archbishopric states that the Ottoman government undertook massacres of Assyrian civilians with "no revolutionary tendencies" in the provinces of Diyarbekir, Urfa, Van, Harput and Bitlis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regarding the number of "Nestorians", which refers to the Assyrian population, killed in Persia and Azerbaijan between 1915 and 1918, R.J. Rummel used Toynbee's and other contemporary reports to calculate a figure of deaths between 46,800 and 47,200. He calculated a death toll of 102,000 Christians in the Assyrian-populated regions of Turkey, but these numbers also included the Armenians and Greeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Assyrians were not subject to genocidal policies to the extent of the Armenians. However, in some provinces, particularly Diyarbakır and Mardin, the Assyrian population was devastated. In other provinces the population was left relatively intact.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Documented_accounts_of_the_genocide">Documented accounts of the genocide</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyriangenocide2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="710" data-file-width="340" height="418" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Assyriangenocide2.jpg/200px-Assyriangenocide2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Assyriangenocide2.jpg/300px-Assyriangenocide2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/38/Assyriangenocide2.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyriangenocide2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An article from <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, March 27, 1915.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Assyrians in what is now Turkey primarily lived in the provinces of <a href="/wiki/Hakkari" title="Hakkari">Hakkari</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C5%9E%C4%B1rnak" title="Şırnak">Şırnak</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mardin" title="Mardin">Mardin</a>. These areas also had a sizable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish</a> population.</p>
<p>The following newspaper articles documented the Assyrian genocide as it occurred:</p>
<ul>
<li>"Assyrians Burned in Church", <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sun_(Lowell)" title="The Sun (Lowell)">The Sun</a></i> (Lowell, Massachusetts), 1915</li>
<li>"Assyrians Massacred in Urmia", <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_San_Antonio_Light" title="The San Antonio Light">The San Antonio Light</a></i> (San Antonio, Texas), 1915</li>
<li>"Assyrians Massacred in Urmiah", <i><a href="/wiki/The_Salt_Lake_Tribune" title="The Salt Lake Tribune">The Salt Lake Tribune</a></i>, 1915</li>
<li>"Chaldean Victims of the Turks", <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>, 22 November 1919, p. 11</li>
<li>"Christian Massacres in Urmiah", <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Argus_(Australia)" title="The Argus (Australia)">The Argus</a></i> (Australia), 1915</li>
<li>"Extermination of the Armenian Race", <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Manchester_Guardian" title="The Manchester Guardian">The Manchester Guardian</a></i>, 1915</li>
<li>"Many Assyrian Perish", <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Winnipeg_Free_Press" title="The Winnipeg Free Press">The Winnipeg Free Press</a></i>, 1915</li>
<li>"Massacred by Kurds; Christians Unable to Flee from Urmia Put to Death", <i><a href="/wiki/The_Washington_Post" title="The Washington Post">The Washington Post</a></i>, 14 March 1915, p. 10</li>
<li>"Massacres of Nestorians in Urmia", <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, 1915</li>
<li>"Massacres Kept Up", <i>The Washington Post</i>, 26 March 1915, p. 1</li>
<li>"Native Christians Massacred; Frightful Atrocities in Persia", <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Los_Angeles_Times" title="The Los Angeles Times">The Los Angeles Times</a></i>, 2 April 1915, p I-1</li>
<li>"Nestorian Christians Flee Urmia", <i>The New York Times</i>, 1915</li>
<li>"Syrian Tells of Atrocities", <i>The Los Angeles Times</i>, Dec. 15, 1918, at I–1.</li>
<li>"The Assyrian Massacres", <i>The Manchester Guardian</i>, Dec. 5, 1918, at 4</li>
<li>"The Suffering Serbs and Armenians", <i>The Manchester Guardian</i>, 1915, p5</li>
<li>"Turkish Horrors in Persia", <i>The New York Times</i>, 11 October 1915</li>
<li>"Turks Kill Christians in Assyria", <i><a href="/wiki/Muscatine_Journal" title="Muscatine Journal">Muscatine Journal</a></i> (Muscatine, Iowa), 1915</li>
<li>"Turkish Troops Massacring Assyrians, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Newark_Advocate" title="The Newark Advocate">The Newark Advocate</a></i>, 1915</li>
<li>"Turkish Horrors in Persia", <i>The New York Times</i>, 1915</li>
<li>"The Total of Armenian and Syrian Dead", <i><a href="/wiki/Current_History" title="Current History">Current History</a>: A Monthly Magazine of the New York Times</i>, November 1916, 337–38</li>
</ul>
<p>Hannibal Travis, Assistant Professor of Law at <a href="/wiki/Florida_International_University" title="Florida International University">Florida International University</a>, wrote in the peer-reviewed journal <i>Genocide Studies and Prevention: An International Journal</i> that:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis_32-7"><a href="#cite_note-Travis-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Numerous articles in the American press documented the genocide of Assyrians by the Turks and their Kurdish allies. By 1918, <i>The Los Angeles Times</i> carried the story of a Syrian, or most likely an Assyrian, merchant from Urmia who stated that his city was "completely wiped out, the inhabitants massacred", 200 surrounding villages ravaged, 200,000 of his people dead, and hundreds of thousands of more starving to death in exile from their agricultural lands. In an article entitled "Native Christians Massacred", the Associated Press correspondent reported that in the vicinity of Urmia, "Turkish regular troops and Kurds are persecuting and massacring Assyrian Christians". Close to 800 were confirmed dead in Urmia, and another 2,000 had perished from disease. Two hundred Assyrians had been burned to death inside a church, and the Russians had discovered more than 700 bodies of massacre victims in the village of Hafdewan outside Urmia, "mostly naked and mutilated", some with gunshot wounds, others decapitated, and still others carved to pieces.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>Other leading British and American newspapers corroborated these accounts of the Assyrian genocide. <i>The New York Times</i> reported on 11 October that 12,000 Persian Christians had died of massacre, hunger, or disease; thousands of girls as young as seven had been raped or forcibly converted to Islam; Christian villages had been destroyed, and three-fourths of these Christian villages were burned to the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> <i>The Times</i> of London was perhaps the first widely respected publication to document the fact that 250,000 Assyrians and Chaldeans eventually died in the Ottoman genocide of Christians, a figure which many journalists and scholars have subsequently accepted.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eyewitness_accounts_and_quotes">Eyewitness accounts and quotes</span></h3>
<p>Statement of German Missionaries on Urmia.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>There was absolutely no human power to protect these unhappy people from the savage onslaught of the invading hostile forces. It was an awful situation. At midnight the terrible exodus began; a concourse of 25,000 men, women, and children, Assyrians and Armenians, leaving cattle in the stables, all their household hoods and all the supply of food for winter, hurried, panic-stricken, on a long and painful journey to the Russian border, enduring the intense privations of a foot journey in the snow and mud, without any kind of preparation. ... It was a dreadful sight, ... many of the old people and children died along the way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The latest news is that four thousand Assyrians and one hundred Armenians have died of disease alone, at the mission, within the last five months. All villages in the surrounding district with two or three exceptions have been plundered and burnt; twenty thousand Christians have been slaughtered in Armenia and its environs. In Haftewan, a village of Salmas, 750 corpses without heads have been recovered from the wells and cisterns alone. Why? Because the commanding officer had put a price on every Christian head... In Dilman crowds of Christians were thrown into prison and driven to accept Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Recognition">Recognition</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan,_Armenia.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1936" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan%2C_Armenia.JPG/190px-Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan%2C_Armenia.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan%2C_Armenia.JPG/285px-Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan%2C_Armenia.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan%2C_Armenia.JPG/380px-Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan%2C_Armenia.JPG 2x" width="190"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_in_Yerevan,_Armenia.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Assyrian Genocide memorial in <a href="/wiki/Yerevan" title="Yerevan">Yerevan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Assyrian historians attribute the limited recognition to the smaller number of Assyrian survivors, whose leader <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mar_Shimun_XXI_Benyamin" title="Mar Shimun XXI Benyamin">Mar Shimun XXI Benyamin</a> was killed in 1918.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Travis_32-8"><a href="#cite_note-Travis-32">[32]</a></sup> For example, there are one million Armenians living in the United States alone, but even they were unable to persuade Congress to pass a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_resolution_on_Armenian_genocide" title="United States resolution on Armenian genocide">United States resolution on Armenian genocide</a>. In addition, the widespread massacres of all <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christians</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> is sometimes referred to by Armenian authors as an "<a href="/wiki/Armenian_Genocide" title="Armenian Genocide">Armenian Genocide</a>".</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Timeline_of_recognition">Timeline of recognition</span></h3>
<ul>
<li>On April 24, 2001, Governor of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">US</a> state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Pataki" title="George Pataki">George Pataki</a>, proclaimed that "killings of civilians and food and water deprivation during forced marches across harsh, arid terrain proved successful for the perpetrators of genocide, who harbored a prejudice against ... Assyrian Christians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></li>
<li>In December 2007, the <a href="/wiki/International_Association_of_Genocide_Scholars" title="International Association of Genocide Scholars">International Association of Genocide Scholars</a>, the world's leading genocide scholars organization, overwhelmingly passed a resolution officially recognizing the Assyrian genocide, along with the genocide against <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Greeks" title="Ottoman Greeks">Ottoman Greeks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> The vote in favor was 83%. The <a href="/wiki/Interparliamentary_Assembly_on_Orthodoxy" title="Interparliamentary Assembly on Orthodoxy">Interparliamentary Assembly on Orthodoxy</a> (I.A.O.), passed a resolution officially recognizing the Assyrian genocide on June 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></li>
<li>On April 2008, <a href="/wiki/David_Paterson" title="David Paterson">David Paterson</a>, the governor of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, recognized the genocide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></li>
<li>On 11 March 2010, the genocide was officially recognized by the <a href="/wiki/Riksdag" title="Riksdag">Riksdag</a> of <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, alongside that of the <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greeks" title="Pontic Greeks">Pontic Greeks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></li>
<li>In May 2013, the Assyrian genocide was recognized by the <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales" title="New South Wales">New South Wales</a> state parliament in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></li>
<li>In March 2015, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> became the second country to recognize the Assyrian genocide in a declaration from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_Armenia" title="National Assembly of Armenia">National Assembly</a> which concurrently recognized the Greek genocide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></li>
<li>In April 2015, the parliaments of both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dutch_parliament" title="Dutch parliament">Netherlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austrian_Parliament" title="Austrian Parliament">Austria</a> also recognized the Assyrian and Greek genocides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></li>
<li>On the 2nd of June, 2016, the <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German</a> <a href="/wiki/Bundestag" title="Bundestag">Bundestag</a> recognized the genocides against the Armenian and Assyrian (also referred to as Syriacs, Chaldeans or Aramaic-speaking Christians) people. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Monuments">Monuments</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_Wall_and_Plaque_at_St._Mary%27s_Parish_in_Tarzana_CA_17February2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1252" data-file-width="1840" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_Wall_and_Plaque_at_St._Mary%27s_Parish_in_Tarzana_CA_17February2007.jpg/220px-Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_Wall_and_Plaque_at_St._Mary%27s_Parish_in_Tarzana_CA_17February2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_Wall_and_Plaque_at_St._Mary%27s_Parish_in_Tarzana_CA_17February2007.jpg/330px-Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_Wall_and_Plaque_at_St._Mary%27s_Parish_in_Tarzana_CA_17February2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_Wall_and_Plaque_at_St._Mary%27s_Parish_in_Tarzana_CA_17February2007.jpg/440px-Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_Wall_and_Plaque_at_St._Mary%27s_Parish_in_Tarzana_CA_17February2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyrian_Genocide_Memorial_Wall_and_Plaque_at_St._Mary%27s_Parish_in_Tarzana_CA_17February2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Memorial wall and plaque at St. Mary's Parish in Tarzana, California.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyrianmonument.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1474" data-file-width="1065" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Assyrianmonument.jpg/170px-Assyrianmonument.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Assyrianmonument.jpg/255px-Assyrianmonument.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Assyrianmonument.jpg/340px-Assyrianmonument.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyrianmonument.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Assyrian Genocide monument in <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a>, Australia.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are monuments commemorating the victims of the Assyrian genocide in <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Belgium" title="Belgium">Belgium</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. Sweden's government has pledged to pay for all the expenses of a future monument, after strong lobbying from the large Assyrian community there, led by Konstantin Sabo. There are three monuments in the U.S., one in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, one in <a href="/wiki/Columbia,_South_Carolina" title="Columbia, South Carolina">Columbia</a> and the newest in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Angeles,_California" title="Los Angeles, California">Los Angeles, California</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>On August 2010, a monument to the victims was built in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fairfield_City" title="Fairfield City">Fairfield City</a> in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, a local government area of <a href="/wiki/Sydney" title="Sydney">Sydney</a> where <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Australians" title="Assyrian Australians">one in ten</a> of the population is of Assyrian descent. Designed by Lewis Batros, the statue is designed as a hand of a martyr draped in an <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_flag" title="Assyrian flag">Assyrian flag</a> and stands at 4.5 meters tall. The memorial <a href="/wiki/Statue" title="Statue">statue</a> was proposed in August 2009. After conference with the community, Fairfield Council received more than 100 submissions for the memorial and two petitions.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:192px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Assyrianmonument.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="701" data-file-width="576" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Assyrianmonument.JPG/190px-Assyrianmonument.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Assyrianmonument.JPG/285px-Assyrianmonument.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Assyrianmonument.JPG/380px-Assyrianmonument.JPG 2x" width="190"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Assyrianmonument.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Genocide monument in Paris, France</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The proposal has been condemned by the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_Australians" title="Turkish Australians">Australian Turkish</a> community. Turkey's consul general to Sydney expressed resentment about the monument, while acknowledging that tragedies had occurred to Assyrians in the period as well as Turks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-73">[73]</a></sup> On the 30th of August 2010, twenty-three days after it was unveiled, the Australian monument was vandalised.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The genocide monument in Sydney, Australia was vandalized again on the 15th of April 2016, with the words "F**k Armenians, Assyrians and Jews" spray painted on the monument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Turkish_perceptions">Turkish perceptions</span></h3>
<p>There are different perceptions in Turkey regarding the Assyrian genocide. The Armenian Turkish newspaper <i>Agos</i> has called the events the "Assyrian Genocide".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Turkish journalist Mehmet Alaca referred to the events as "Seyfo" in his 2012 article in <i><a href="/wiki/Radikal" title="Radikal">Radikal</a></i> and wrote that tens of thousands of Assyrians were murdered and expelled at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>However, historian Bülent Özdemir of the <a href="/wiki/Bal%C4%B1kesir_University" title="Balıkesir University">Balıkesir University</a> has pointed out to an "Assyrian rebellion" in Midyat in 1915, and said that the Ottoman Empire cannot be accused of committing a genocide against the Assyrians in any way, claiming that this was supported by documents in foreign archives. He claimed that the fabrication of such a genocide was part of an Assyrian identity-building process. In 2007, Turkish Assyrian Mıtra Hazail Soumi classified the events not as a genocide, but as a massacre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Adriaan Wolvaardt wrote that "Turks view Assyrian allegations as unfounded, unproven and an attack on Turkish national identity" and that "Turks reject the Assyrian claims based on the stigma associated with the concept of genocide and their understanding of Turkish history".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
